**Total Estimated Effort:** 138h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £3,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify every point where the application uses biometric sign-in during its login process.
- [ ] Set up the organisation’s identity provider (IdP) and authentication rules to limit unsuccessful biometric attempts to a maximum of 5 (ac-07.03_odp).
- [ ] Implement documented behaviour after the limit is reached to block any further biometric attempts and route users to the approved alternative authentication method.
- [ ] Enable and check audit logs for every failed biometric attempt and for a separate event when a threshold is reached and access is blocked.
- [ ] Apply the configuration using configuration management (policy-as-code and infrastructure as code where available) and keep the evidence exports that show the limit is set to 5.
- [ ] Update the biometric sign-in procedures, the system design documentation, and the system security plan to reference the limit of five attempts and what happens after that limit is reached.


---

#### AC-7.4 — Use of Alternate Authentication Factor (Enhancement)

This requirement ensures people have a safe backup way to prove who they are if they mistype their usual login too many times, while preventing attackers from trying repeatedly without limit. Without it, a person could lock themselves out, and a wrongdoer could keep guessing passwords or codes until they eventually succeed—particularly if the system allows unlimited retries using backup methods.

The organisation must allow users to use only two approved alternate ways to sign in when those alternate methods are different from the user’s usual method: either an approved hardware security key or a one-time code generated by an approved authenticator app. These alternate methods can be used only after the organisation’s set number of consecutive failed attempts for the usual method has been exceeded.

When a user is signing in using an alternate method, the system must block further attempts after 5 consecutive failed attempts within any rolling 15-minute period, and it must count failures during the alternate sign-in process.

The organisation must document how this works, configure the system accordingly, and keep audit records showing the failed attempts and their timing so the approach can be checked.

##### IdP alternate-factor recovery with 5-in-15 rolling throttle

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Confirm IdP capabilities and current authentication journey configuration (primary vs alternate factor classification points, recovery/lockout flow, available factor types) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement IdP policy logic for alternate-factor eligibility (alternate factors permitted only after primary-factor consecutive invalid threshold is exceeded; ensure alternate method must be different from primary) | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Configure approved alternate factors only (enable FIDO2/WebAuthn security keys and authenticator-app OTP; explicitly disable/withhold other MFA methods for this recovery path) | IAM engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement rolling throttle for alternate attempts (5 consecutive invalid alternate-factor attempts within any rolling 15-minute window; counter increments per failed alternate attempt; block/unblock behaviour tied to rolling window) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Validate auditability and event semantics (eligibility timestamp, each failed alternate attempt with timestamps, and block/unblock events; ensure counters are separate and reset behaviour matches rolling-window requirements) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| SIEM integration and detection readiness (map IdP events to SIEM fields; create/adjust alerts/dashboards for alternate-factor throttling and repeated failures; confirm log retention meets audit needs) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 20h | £3,000 |
| Build and review test plan + evidence pack (test cases for eligibility trigger, same-method bypass prevention, factor restrictions, rolling-window edge cases, and block lifting; include screenshots/log excerpts for audit) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Produce helpdesk/user runbooks and operational guidance (how to recover using approved alternates; how to verify whether recovery is currently permitted; explain 15-minute rolling-window impact; include escalation steps) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 156h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £3,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] For each authentication journey, identify the main factor used for sign-in, and specify which approved alternative methods can be used when an alternative is different from the main factor.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider (IdP) policy so that recovery using an alternative factor is allowed only after the number of consecutive invalid attempts at the primary factor exceeds the threshold set by the organisation.
- [ ] Limit the backup recovery method to exactly FIDO2/Web Authentication (FIDO2/WebAuthn) security keys and one-time passwords (OTPs) from an authentication app.
- [ ] Set up a rolling limit that blocks alternating-factor attempts after 5 invalid attempts in any rolling 15-minute period.
- [ ] Check that the “alternate attempt” failure counter increases after every failed alternate authentication attempt, and that it only resets once the rolling time window has fully passed.
- [ ] Enable and export audit logs for primary failures, alternate eligibility checks, every failed alternate attempt, and block and unblock events to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Publish and approve helpdesk and user runbooks that describe the recovery steps and the effect of the 15-minute rolling time window.


---

### AC-8 — System Use Notification (Control)

This requirement ensures people are shown a clear warning before they enter a government system, so they understand what they are agreeing to. Without this, users could access the system without realising that their activity may be monitored and recorded, and that unauthorised use is not allowed and can result in criminal and civil penalties—raising the risk of misuse and privacy harm.

The organisation must show the warning before any user can sign in through a human sign-in screen. The warning must state that it is a U.S. Government system, that use may be monitored, recorded, and reviewed, that unauthorised use is prohibited and subject to penalties, and that using the system means they consent to monitoring and recording. The message must remain on screen until the user acknowledges the conditions and takes the explicit step to log on or continue.

If the system is publicly accessible, it must also display a public notice before further access. This notice must explain that monitoring and recording are limited to security and fraud prevention, that no content is used for unauthorised profiling, and that only authorised uses are permitted. It must also include any privacy-consistent limits and a description of authorised uses.

The organisation must have the warning text approved, configure the system so the notice appears and cannot be skipped, and keep records showing that users acknowledged the warning and proceeded.

##### IdP pre-auth warning with explicit acknowledgement gate

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Produce AC-8 banner implementation design for IdP pre-auth acknowledgement gate (including timing requirement, bypass prevention, and evidence fields) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Legal and privacy approval pack: submit system use notification and public access notice text for review; obtain documented approvals and create controlled approval record | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create controlled configuration items: versioned banner content (system use + public access), including required privacy-consistent limits and authorised-use statement | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Implement IdP acknowledgement gate in authentication flow (required pre-auth step, explicit checkbox + “Acknowledge and continue”, block token/session issuance until acknowledgement) | IAM Engineer | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Implement multi-language rendering (where required) using approved translations and ensure banner meaning/content consistency across languages | IAM Engineer | Business Unit | 16h | — |
| Configure audit logging and evidence capture (user identifier, timestamp, banner version/hash, acknowledgement completion) and forward to SIEM/log retention with searchable fields | SIEM/Logging Engineer | Department | 24h | £3,000 |
| Test, validate, and produce audit-ready evidence (functional tests for bypass attempts, timing verification, acknowledgement persistence, and log completeness across environments) | Quality Assurance Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 156h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £3,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Get documented approval from Privacy and Legal for the system use notification and any public access notice.
- [ ] Create controlled, versioned banner templates for the United States Government warning and the public access notice.
- [ ] Set up the identity provider (IdP) sign-in process to show the banner before authentication, and prevent users from continuing until they explicitly acknowledge it.
- [ ] Make sure the banner is displayed before the identity provider issues the session or token that allows access to the application.
- [ ] Where needed, enable rendering in multiple languages using approved translations, following the same approval process.
- [ ] Check that the identity provider (IdP) logs show when the acknowledgement has been completed, including the banner version or hash, the user, and the time it happened, and then send those logs to security information and event management (SIEM) for long-term storage.

### AC-9 — Previous Logon Notification (Control)

This requirement is about showing users the date and time of their last successful login immediately after they sign in. This is important because, if the system never displays this information, a user may not notice something suspicious—such as an unexpected login—which could indicate misuse of their account or unauthorised activity.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that whenever a person signs in through a normal login screen (for example, a website or a desktop sign-in), the system immediately shows a message that includes the date and time of that person’s most recent successful login. This should be based on the organisation’s stored records of successful logins, not on failed sign-in attempts.

The organisation should also document how this works, configure the system so the message appears on all supported login screens, and be able to provide evidence that the correct date and time are shown after successful sign-in.

##### Show last successful logon time in IdP sign-in experience

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Confirm all supported interactive human authentication paths for the application are routed via the IdP (web portal, SSO-enabled clients, any alternate login entry points) and document any exceptions | IAM engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Review IdP capabilities for “previous/last successful sign-in” messaging and select the exact configuration mechanism (sign-in customisation/template, policy rule, or equivalent) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Configure IdP sign-in flow to display last successful logon date/time **only after successful authentication**, including conditional rendering to suppress the message on failed/denied attempts | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Bind the displayed timestamp to the IdP’s authoritative “last successful sign-in” attribute/record and validate it updates only on successful logons (no failed attempts, no lock/disabled account updates) | IAM engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Standardise date/time presentation (UK local time vs UTC, format, locale) and configure the IdP template/message accordingly; ensure consistent output across supported browsers/clients | Architect | Enterprise | 8h | — |
| Implement audit/evidence linkage: ensure IdP audit logs include user identifier and last successful sign-in timestamp; define retention and produce a repeatable evidence capture approach (e.g., screenshot + corresponding audit event reference) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Execute acceptance testing including negative cases (failed logons, locked/disabled accounts) and produce audit-ready evidence for at least one representative user in test and (if required) pre-prod | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 108h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Confirm that all interactive human log-in routes for the application are authenticated through the organisation’s identity provider (IdP).
- [ ] Enable identity provider (IdP) messaging in the interactive sign-in flow to show the user’s “previous sign-in” and “last successful sign-in” information.
- [ ] Set the sign-in template to show the identity provider’s (IdP) last successful sign-in date and time only after the user has been successfully authenticated.
- [ ] Standardise and set the time zone and time format used in notifications.
- [ ] Set up automated collection of proof (for example, capture a screenshot of the sign-in page and link it to the identity provider (IdP) audit log entry).
- [ ] Run acceptance tests to confirm that logins succeed, that failed logins are handled correctly, and that locked or disabled accounts are blocked; record the results.

#### AC-9.1 — Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

This expectation helps a person notice suspicious or unexpected sign-in behaviour. Immediately after they sign in successfully, the system should show them how many times their sign-in attempts have failed since their last successful sign-in. Without this, a user may not realise that someone else is trying to access their account, or they may not be able to tell whether repeated failures match their own activity. This can delay reporting and increase the risk of unauthorised access.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- Keep a running count for each user of failed (rejected) sign-in attempts that occur after their last successful sign-in and before their next successful sign-in.
- Increase the count each time the system rejects incorrect sign-in details.
- Reset the count to zero immediately after a successful sign-in.

Whenever the user signs in successfully, the system must show that user only the correct number of failed attempts for the most recent period.

##### Per-user failed-logon counter with post-success user notification

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Design per-user failed-logon counter model (keying strategy, atomic increment/reset semantics, concurrency approach, and authentication-context scoping) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement counter storage and event-handling logic in the enterprise authentication flow (increment on failure, atomic read+reset on success) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Add auditable logging for increments and the success-time counter value/reset (SIEM-friendly fields, correlation IDs, and retention alignment) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement post-success user notification rendering (only to the authenticated user; correct correlation to the immediately preceding failure window; safe templating) | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Configure authentication event hooks/webhooks and wiring to the application/IdP post-login experience (including test vs production environments) | Infrastructure Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Develop and execute test plan (unit/integration tests, race-condition tests, negative tests for cross-user leakage, and evidence capture for audit) | Quality Assurance Lead | Department | 24h | — |
| Operational readiness: monitoring/alerting for counter anomalies, runbook updates, and deployment/rollback plan | Operations Lead | Department | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 152h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up the per-user counter data structure and the method for choosing each user’s key, using the user identifier from the application and the identity provider (for example, the subject or username).
- [ ] In the authentication event handler, increase the user’s failure count by 1 in a way that is fully reliable and cannot be partially completed, for every unsuccessful authentication attempt.
- [ ] After every successful authentication, perform an “all-or-nothing” read-and-reset so the notification value always reflects the most recent failure period.
- [ ] Add a post-login user notification that shows the counter value immediately after a successful logon.
- [ ] Only show notification content for the currently authenticated session of the user who has just logged in.
- [ ] Set up and test audit records for counter increases, the selection of the success-time value, and reset actions, and send them to the security information and event management (SIEM) system with matching reference identifiers.
- [ ] Set up automated tests that deliberately recreate failure scenarios, and check that the shown count and the reset behaviour work correctly even when multiple attempts happen at the same time.

#### AC-9.2 — Successful and Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

This requirement helps people notice unusual sign-in activity. Right after a user successfully signs in, the system must show how many sign-in attempts happened recently—specifically, it must display the number of both successful sign-ins and unsuccessful sign-in attempts during the previous 24 hours.  

Without this, a user may not realise their account is being targeted, or may not notice that the pattern of sign-ins does not match what they actually did. This could mean unauthorised access goes unnoticed for longer.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure the sign-in system to count both successful and failed attempts over the last 24 hours, based on the time of the user’s successful sign-in, and then show both numbers together in the message the user sees immediately after they log on.

##### Post-login notification of last-24h successful and failed logons

| Design rolling 24-hour counting logic per successful logon event (window boundaries, concurrency handling, idempotency/deduplication strategy) and produce implementation specification | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement server-side post-auth handler to compute `success_count_last_24h` and `unsuccess_count_last_24h` using event timestamps, including performance safeguards (caching/batching where appropriate) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Implement UI/session injection to render the notification immediately after successful login (web/mobile/applicable landing experience), including secure templating and localisation-ready strings | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 24h | — |

- [ ] Turn on and confirm reliable recording of authentication events in the integrated identity service, for both successful and unsuccessful sign-in attempts.
- [ ] Set up a post-login process that runs after a successful logon and calculates, using the event times, how many logons occurred in the previous 24 hours on a rolling basis.
- [ ] Define and document what counts as an “unsuccessful sign-in attempt” for the organisation’s sign-in process (for example, incorrect credentials and multi-factor authentication (MFA) failure), using the event types that are available.
- [ ] Insert the calculated counts into the user’s immediate post-login experience (user interface or session details) and show one notification that includes both figures.
- [ ] Add server-side recording of evidence for each successful logon notification that is generated, including the start and end times of the search window and the calculated totals.
- [ ] Carry out controlled login attempts to confirm the notification appears only after a successful login, and that the counts match the figures from the last 24 hours.

#### AC-9.3 — Notification of Account Changes (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure users are told, immediately after they successfully sign in, about any important changes made to their account in the previous 24 hours. This matters because if someone changes your password, changes how you prove it is really you, or quietly changes what you can access, you may not notice and could continue using a compromised account without realising.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must, every time a user successfully logs on, check what security-related changes were recorded for that user during the past 24 hours and include them in a notification. This includes changes to how multi-step sign-in is set up, any password reset activity, any changes to what the user is allowed to do or which groups or roles they belong to, and any changes to security rules or role assignments.

The notification must be based on the organisation’s recorded system information, follow the organisation’s own sign-in notification procedures, and be properly documented, configured, and verifiable using audit records and the organisation’s security plan.

| Design the sign-in-time digest workflow (time window logic, event filtering rules, digest schema, spoofing-resistant in-session rendering approach) | Architect | Enterprise | 20h | — |

- [ ] Set up the digest generation process so it includes only the security-related characteristics and parameters defined by the organisation.
- [ ] Set up an in-session post-login notification to show the digest immediately after a successful logon.

#### AC-9.4 — Additional Logon Information (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure that, immediately after a successful login, users are shown where their account was last used and what device they are currently using. This matters because if an account is accessed from an unexpected location or device, the user may not notice quickly, which could allow unauthorised access or fraud to continue for longer than necessary.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must display an immediate on-screen message after a successful login. The message must include:
- the last successful login location, including the internet address and an approximate city or region, and
- the device identifier,

using the organisation’s agreed wording and content.

The organisation must also:
- have documented procedures and system design that explain how this information is collected and shown,
- configure the system so it can reliably produce the message,
- keep records that can be checked later to confirm the message was generated and/or displayed, and
- include this approach in its overall system security plan.

- [ ] Use an approved location method to estimate the city or region from the last successful sign-in IP address.
- [ ] Map and include a stable device identifier claim linked to the most recent successful sign-in, and define an organisation-approved fallback for devices that are not managed.
- [ ] Add application and identity provider (IdP) logging that records: the successful logon, the notification details that were calculated, and confirmation that the notification was shown.
- [ ] Update the system design documentation and the system security plan to explain how the notification is created and what audit evidence is kept.

### AC-10 — Concurrent Session Control (Control)

This requirement is about preventing powerful accounts from being used in too many places at the same time. It does this by limiting each privileged system account (such as domain administrators and local administrators) and each service account used by mission-critical applications to a maximum of 2 simultaneous sessions.

This matters because, if these accounts can be used without limits, mistakes or unauthorised access are more likely to spread. That makes misuse harder to spot and increases the damage if any account is compromised.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must:
- clearly define which accounts are covered,
- set the maximum to 2 concurrent sessions per covered account, and
- ensure the systems actively block any attempt to create a third simultaneous session for those accounts.

The organisation must also:
- document the rule and the day-to-day steps for applying and checking it,
- describe how the system is designed to enforce it,
- configure the relevant settings,
- keep evidence that the settings are in place,
- include this approach in its security planning, and
- be able to show, through testing and records, that the limit is consistently enforced.

##### Enforce 2 concurrent sessions for privileged and service accounts

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Produce authoritative scoping model for covered accounts (privileged system accounts + mission-critical service accounts) including deterministic mapping (groups/attributes) and environment-aware rules | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement IdP concurrency policy for covered accounts: set max 2 concurrent sessions per account and define deterministic third-session behaviour (deny and/or terminate oldest) | IAM Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Configure/validate application access paths to ensure session decisions are enforced via IdP (SSO/OIDC/SAML) and identify any non-IdP access gaps | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Where IdP cannot enforce directly, implement equivalent enforcement on access broker/jump host (e.g., gateway session brokering/termination aligned to IdP policy) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | £6,000 |
| Enable audit-ready logging and SIEM forwarding for successful sessions and blocked/terminated third-session attempts; define alerting thresholds and retention expectations | SOC / Monitoring Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Create configuration evidence pack (policy definitions, concurrency settings, scoping mappings, exports/policy-as-code references) suitable for AC-10 audit review | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Execute repeatable test plan per covered account type (Session A/B succeed; Session C blocked/terminated) and produce signed test evidence + remediation notes | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Publish operational runbook for handling legitimate temporary exceptions (process, approvals, how to verify current session counts, rollback steps) | Service Delivery Manager | Enterprise | 8h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 132h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £6,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create and keep up to date an official identity provider (IdP) mapping for the privileged system accounts and mission-critical service accounts that are in scope, based on group membership and/or account attributes.
- [ ] Set up identity provider (IdP) session controls so that each covered account can have no more than 2 active sessions at the same time.
- [ ] Set consistent third-session behaviour (either deny access or end the session) and record what operations should expect to happen.
- [ ] Check that all relevant ways to access the covered accounts—both for applications and administrative functions—go through the identity provider (IdP), or through an access broker that applies the same access limits.
- [ ] Turn on and send logs about session creation and any blocked or terminated attempts to create multiple concurrent sessions to the security information and event management (SIEM) system for audit and monitoring.
- [ ] Export and store configuration evidence (policy settings and identity scoping) in configuration management and policy-as-code, and run a repeatable test to confirm that Session A and Session B succeed, and that Session C is blocked or terminated.


---

### AC-11 — Device Lock (Control)

This requirement ensures that when a person steps away from a work computer or work app, the system is not left accessible to anyone nearby. If it is not enforced, a passer-by could open files, view sensitive information, or continue using the person’s already logged-in work session, creating a real risk of data exposure or misuse.

The organisation must automatically lock the user’s device after 15 minutes of inactivity. While the device is locked, it must not allow any further access. The device must stay locked until the user returns and re-establishes access using the organisation’s normal sign-in method, such as entering their usual password or personal code.

The organisation must also make sure that locking does not replace logging out when users are required to end their session, for example at the end of the workday.

##### Enforce 15-minute inactivity device lock with re-authentication

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Confirm target platforms and endpoint management scope (Windows/macOS/Linux, device types, kiosk/shared devices, privileged vs standard users) and define the 15-minute inactivity lock policy mapping | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 8h | — |
| Configure managed endpoint inactivity lock timer to 15 minutes and enforce secure lock screen/secure desktop behaviour via endpoint management baselines (including drift-resistant settings) | Infrastructure Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Enforce re-authentication on unlock using the organisation’s normal sign-in method (e.g., password/PIN/SSO-backed sign-in) and disable any “resume without sign-in”/bypass paths | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Validate and harden against proximity/auto-unlock and weak unlock behaviours (e.g., disable/limit trusted devices, Bluetooth/Wi‑Fi unlock, wake-to-unlock exceptions) with documented exceptions process | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Implement centrally managed drift control and continuous compliance auditing (configuration reports, scheduled checks, alerting on non-compliance, evidence capture) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 12h | £3,000 |
| Create audit-ready evidence pack and test results (screenshots/config exports, audit logs, sample device compliance reports, re-authentication test scripts and outcomes) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 10h | — |
| Reinforce “not a substitute for logout” through endpoint/app configuration guardrails and operational policy (e.g., disable persistent sessions for sensitive apps where required; confirm app session timeout/logout alignment) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 10h | £2,500 |
| Rollout plan execution: staged deployment, change management records, user guidance, and post-deployment verification across device groups | Project Manager | Enterprise | 14h | — |
| **Total Estimated Effort** |  |  | **98h** |  |
| **Total Estimated Cost** |  |  |  | **£5,500** |

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set the endpoint operating system to lock the session after 15 minutes of inactivity.
- [ ] Require a normal sign-in (password, personal identification number (PIN), smart card, or single sign-on (SSO)) to unlock, and prevent unlocking without signing in.
- [ ] Disable or limit any “nearby” or “auto-unlock” options that would let someone bypass the normal re-authentication (re-login) process.
- [ ] Apply the configuration to all in-scope user devices using the organisation’s endpoint management platform.
- [ ] Set up ongoing compliance checks and alerts to detect changes (for example, the lock timer is not set to 15 minutes, or an unlock bypass option is enabled).
- [ ] Publish and put into practice guidance that device lock does not replace logging out at the end of the day or when a policy requires it. Also check that sensitive applications follow the logout requirement.


---

#### AC-11.1 — Pattern-hiding Displays (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring that, as soon as someone locks their computer or phone, anything that was previously shown on the screen is immediately hidden from view and replaced with a harmless display. Without this, someone nearby could quickly look at the screen and read sensitive information that was left open, such as parts of a document, email, or other controlled material, even though the device is “locked”.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure devices so that the lock action immediately conceals the earlier screen content and shows only an approved, publicly viewable display. This approved display can be, for example: a pattern, a screen-saver style image, a photograph, a solid colour, a clock or battery indicator, or a blank screen.

The organisation must document how the locking process works, record the relevant device settings and how this behaviour is built into the system, and be able to provide evidence that the screen remains hidden and does not briefly show (“ghost”) the previous content during or after locking.

##### Enforce approved lock-screen pattern-hiding on managed endpoints

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Confirm supported endpoint OS/device types and define the approved lock-screen display policy (allowed image types, preview/widget restrictions, and inactivity/manual lock requirements) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Produce the MDM/UEM configuration baseline (profiles/settings) to enforce approved lock-screen behaviour using OS-native lock mechanisms (including disabling/limiting lock-screen previews) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement “lock transition hardening” settings where available (e.g., disable quick glance/notification previews on lock, ensure full-cover lock screen, and prevent framebuffer/ghosting artefacts) | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 20h | — |
| Configure and test manual lock + inactivity lock triggers across representative endpoint builds; document observed behaviour and confirm approved lock display appears immediately and persists for the locked state | Operations Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Create audit-ready evidence pack: export configuration/profile baselines from the management platform and record repeatable test results (including first-seconds verification) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Implement drift monitoring and alerting for non-compliant endpoints (e.g., wrong lock image type, previews enabled, or profile not applied) and define remediation workflow | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 20h | £3,000 |
| Roll out the configuration to pilot groups, remediate exceptions, then schedule enterprise-wide deployment with change management sign-off | Project Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 144h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £3,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Choose and record the approved lock-screen display options allowed for AC-11(1): pattern or screen-saver, photograph, solid colour, clock and battery indicator, or a blank screen.
- [ ] Set up centrally managed device controls (mobile device management and unified endpoint management) to enforce the approved lock-screen display and turn off lock-screen previews and widgets that could show sensitive information.
- [ ] Set up endpoints so they automatically lock the user session when a user manually triggers it and when there is inactivity, in line with organisational standards.
- [ ] Check the lock change on sample endpoint builds to confirm there is no “ghosting” or content bleeding through, and that the approved image shows immediately after the lock is applied.
- [ ] Export and keep endpoint configuration baseline reports from the management platform as evidence for audits.
- [ ] Set up compliance monitoring and alerts for devices that change from the approved lock-screen settings, and fix any devices that do not meet the requirements.

### AC-12 — Session Termination (Control)

This requirement ensures that a user’s access session is automatically ended when it is no longer needed—for example, after 30 minutes of inactivity, immediately if an account is locked or suspected to be compromised, and during the system’s defined out-of-hours period for non-privileged users. Without this, someone could continue using an unattended or incorrectly accessed account for longer than intended, increasing the risk of unauthorised actions, data exposure, or damage before anyone notices.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must clearly define what counts as inactivity, what events mean “account locked” and “suspected compromise”, set the out-of-hours schedule for non-privileged users, and ensure the system automatically applies these rules. When a session ends, all activity linked to that session must stop, except for any tasks the user has specifically set up to continue.

The organisation must also document the process, include it in its access rules, keep records of the relevant settings and design, maintain a list of the trigger events, and log when session endings occur and the reason why.

##### Enforce AC-12 session termination via IdP session policies

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---|---:|---:|
| Confirm IdP session model and map AC-12 triggers to IdP capabilities (idle timeout, account lock/disable events, risk/compromise signals, off-hours conditional access) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Configure IdP interactive session idle timeout to 30 minutes and validate “inactivity” mapping (no authenticated user actions/requests) across web/mobile/portal flows | IAM Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement immediate termination on account lockout/disable: configure event-driven token/session revocation and sign-out for affected users; document operational tolerance (e.g., propagation time) | IAM Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement immediate termination on suspected compromise: integrate SOC/SOAR risk signals to IdP revocation actions (disable account / revoke refresh tokens / invalidate active sessions) and define the organisation “suspected compromise” condition mapping | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Implement off-hours session termination for non-privileged users using conditional access/session policies with role/group scoping (exclude privileged roles/groups) and validate schedule behaviour | IAM Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Update application session handling to treat IdP session invalidation as authoritative (deny access on revoked/expired tokens; remove reliance on long-lived app sessions; ensure re-authentication) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Implement audit evidence: ensure IdP logs include termination timestamp and trigger cause; configure SIEM forwarding and create an audit-ready report/dashboard for AC-12 evidence | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |

| Total Estimated Effort |  |  | **204h** |  |
| Total Estimated Cost |  |  |  | **£0** |

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Confirm that every way a user can interact with the application requires sign-in through the organisation’s identity provider (OpenID Connect (OIDC) or Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML)), and remove or disable separate long-lasting application sessions where possible.
- [ ] Set the identity provider (IdP) interactive session idle timeout to 30 minutes to enforce the ac-12_odp inactivity trigger.
- [ ] Set up identity provider (IdP)-driven immediate session termination when a user account is locked or disabled.
- [ ] Use security operations centre (SOC) and security orchestration, automation and response (SOAR) “suspected compromise” alerts to revoke affected users’ identity provider (IdP) sessions and tokens.
- [ ] Set time-based access and session rules to end user sessions outside working hours for non-privileged user groups or roles.
- [ ] Update application session handling so access ends when the identity provider’s (IdP) sessions or tokens are revoked or expire, with no continued access through cached sessions.
- [ ] Enable and send identity provider (IdP) audit logs to the security information and event management (SIEM) system. These logs must record when a session ended and why it ended, so they can be used as evidence.

#### AC-12.1 — User-initiated Logouts (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people can end their own signed-in sessions whenever they want, whether they are using a work computer, a password-protected website, or a signed-in database connection. Without an easy logout option that users can trigger themselves, someone could accidentally leave access open, or a session could stay active longer than intended—raising the risk that unauthorised people could use the same access to view or change information.  

To meet this expectation, the organisation must provide a clear logout option that normal users can choose whenever they need to for each of these signed-in session types. When a user selects logout, the session must actually end, so they cannot use it again unless they sign in again.  

The organisation must also have documented procedures for how logout is handled, ensure the system is set up to support this, provide a user-facing confirmation that the session has ended, and record logout and session-ending events in its audit records so the action can be checked later.

##### User-initiated logout across web sessions and SSO-backed clients

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Implement standard “Log out/Sign out” UX trigger across web UI (profile/account menu) and ensure it initiates IdP logout (OIDC RP-initiated logout and/or SAML SLO as applicable) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Implement server-side session termination: invalidate application session store entries and clear session cookies with correct domain/path scoping and `HttpOnly`/`Secure` attributes | Platform Engineer | Department | 20h | — |
| Implement token-based access shutdown: revoke/rotate refresh tokens where supported by IdP; ensure access/refresh cannot silently re-establish a session after logout | Security Engineer | Department | 28h | — |
| Enforce API/web logout consistency: reject requests without a valid active session; ensure bearer tokens are not accepted post-logout (via revocation/blacklisting strategy or strict session binding) | Software Lead | Department | 24h | — |
| Add user-facing logout confirmation page/message and ensure redirect to public landing page occurs after logout completes (including error/timeout handling) | Operations Lead | Team | 8h | — |
| Implement audit logging for logout/session termination and forward to SIEM: include user identifier, session/token reference, timestamp, relying party/client identifier, and source IP; add correlation identifiers | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Create automated tests and evidence: integration tests for “logout then access denied” for web and API; document expected IdP/application behaviour and produce audit-ready runbook updates | Quality Assurance Lead | Business Unit | 32h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 160h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Set up a user-facing **“Log out / Sign out”** button that starts the identity provider’s (IdP) sign-out process for the application.
- [ ] When the user logs out, end any active server-side application sessions and remove all session cookies (using the correct settings for where the cookies apply and their security protections).
- [ ] Set up and enforce the end of user sessions and access tokens so that, after a user logs out, refreshed access cannot be used to sign them back in (based on what the identity provider supports).
- [ ] Add controls to the application programming interface (API) to block requests after a user logs out, by using invalidated session cookies and/or tokens that have been cancelled (revoked).
- [ ] Show a logout confirmation message or page after the session has been ended successfully.
- [ ] Send audit records for user logout and session ending to security information and event management (SIEM), including: user ID, session or token reference, date and time, client or application ID, and source IP address.
- [ ] Create automated tests to prove that access after logout is blocked until the user signs in again (re-authenticates).

#### AC-12.2 — Termination Message (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure people can clearly tell when their login session has ended. In everyday terms, if a system simply disconnects you without a clear message, it may not be obvious whether you were properly logged out. This can cause confusion, mistakes, or the risk that someone else could use a session you thought had finished.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must ensure that when a user’s authenticated session ends, the system displays a clear logout message that confirms the session has ended, rather than only indicating that the connection was lost.

For web browser access, the message should be shown after the session ends. For file transfer sessions such as File Transfer Protocol (FTP), the message should be sent and displayed as the final message before the session is closed.

The organisation should also have documented procedures, defined system behaviour for different session types, the correct system settings, and records showing that session termination and the logout message happened as required.

##### Explicit logout/termination message after session end (web + FTP)

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define session-type behaviour and timing requirements (Web vs FTP) including ordering rules for “termination confirmed” vs “final protocol message” | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Web logout orchestration: implement server-side session invalidation and post-logout message gating (block/withhold message if session still valid) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 40h | — |
| Web SSO (OIDC/SAML) logout flow alignment: ensure IdP/app termination occurs before redirecting to termination message page; add correlation IDs for evidence linkage | Architect | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| FTP server configuration: enable explicit authenticated logout/termination reply as final protocol message and prevent silent disconnects for normal logout | Infrastructure Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Audit evidence instrumentation: add structured logs for (1) session termination timestamp and (2) message displayed/sent timestamp, with correlation IDs for both web and FTP | Security Engineer | Department | 24h | — |
| Automated regression tests: cover normal logout, token already expired, network retry/partial logout, and verify correct ordering of termination vs message for web and FTP | Quality Assurance Lead | Team | 32h | — |
| Operational runbook and troubleshooting guidance: document expected behaviour, failure modes, log queries, and escalation triggers | Operations Lead | Department | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 184h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Document how session logouts work for different services (web versus File Transfer Protocol (FTP)), including the required timing of logout messages in relation to when the session ends.
- [ ] Set up server-side invalidation of web sessions, and only show the “logged out” termination message after the session invalidation has been confirmed.
- [ ] Coordinate single sign-on (SSO) sign-out for OpenID Connect (OIDC) and Security Assertion Markup Language (SAML) so the identity provider (IdP) and application session are fully ended before redirecting to the sign-out confirmation page.
- [ ] Configure the File Transfer Protocol (FTP) server to send a clear logout/termination message as the final message before it closes the authenticated session.
- [ ] Add linked audit records for when a session ends and for when a message is shown or sent (including the date and time and the session identifier).
- [ ] Set up and run automated tests to confirm the correct sequence of messages: the web message must be sent after termination, and the final FTP message must be sent before the connection is closed.

#### AC-12.3 — Timeout Warning Message (Enhancement)

This requirement is about giving people a clear warning before their sign-in session ends, so they are not unexpectedly cut off. Without a specific warning, users could lose work, miss important deadlines, or be confused about why they were logged out. This can also increase the risk of mistakes or unsafe behaviour if people try to work around the problem.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must configure the system so that, during an active session, it shows a clear message stating that the session will end in 5 minutes, using the organisation’s configured 5-minute timing. The message must appear before the logout happens, be easy for a typical user to understand, and ideally encourage the user to continue the session.

The organisation must also have documented procedures explaining how sessions are ended and when the 5-minute warning is shown. It must keep records and documentation that prove the warning timing and that sessions end after the expected period.

##### Session end warning message aligned to configured timeout

- Category: Software
- Priority: High

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|

| Design warning trigger logic to display only while user remains authenticated and ensure it renders before termination (including handling for clock drift and edge cases) | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Implement warning UI component (modal/banner) with plain-language message “Your session will end in X minutes” and a clear “Continue session” prompt | Software Lead | Business Unit | 32h | — |
| Implement “continue” behaviour to perform legitimate session extension or reauthentication consistent with configured session policy (no simple dismissal); include client-side and server-side flow updates | Software Lead | Business Unit | 32h | — |
| Add audit evidence logging for (1) warning displayed/threshold reached and (2) session termination time; ensure logs are correlated with user/session identifiers | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Integrate/export audit logs to enterprise SIEM/logging platform and validate end-to-end evidence availability for test cases | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 16h | £3,000 |
| QA timing validation across supported client types (web/mobile/API flows as applicable), including tolerance checks (±30 seconds) and regression testing; produce test evidence pack | Quality Assurance Lead | Business Unit | 24h | — |
| Produce configuration and procedure documentation (where timeout/offset are set, how warning triggers, how continue/reauth works, how termination occurs) and store artefacts for audit | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 204h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £3,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Identify which system controls how long an authenticated session lasts before logout (identity provider, application, or gateway) and confirm that this is governed by the AC-12 settings.
- [ ] Set the session warning timing so the warning appears exactly {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} before the session ends.
- [ ] Show a warning banner or pop-up message to users that says their session will end in {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} and asks them to choose whether to continue.
- [ ] Set up the “continue” action so it extends the session in line with the policy, or re-checks the user’s identity (not just dismiss the warning).
- [ ] Add logging to record the time when the warning threshold is reached or shown, and the time when the session ends, and send this information to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Create and securely store audit-ready proof of configuration (for example, policy exports or configuration snapshots), and document how sessions are ended and how warnings are issued.
- [ ] Carry out a controlled test to measure the time between the warning and the logout, and confirm it matches {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }} within the agreed tolerance.

### AC-14 — Permitted Actions Without Identification or Authentication (Control)

This requirement is about ensuring that only a clearly defined set of actions can be carried out on a system before anyone has proved who they are or signed in. Examples include viewing public information pages, receiving inbound calls on a mobile phone, receiving facsimiles, and performing emergency maintenance using a protected physical bypass switch. Without this clear list, unauthorised people could try to use the system, which could lead to information being exposed, services being disrupted, or emergency access being misused.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must identify exactly which actions are allowed without authentication (before identity checks and login) and confirm that each one supports the organisation’s mission and day-to-day business needs. It must then record in the system’s security plan both:
- the exact list of these permitted unauthenticated actions, and
- a plain-language explanation for why identity checks and login are not required for each action,

making sure that no normal restricted activities are included.

##### Define and document permitted unauthenticated actions per system

- Category: Manual
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Confirm the system boundary and starting point for AC-14 unauthenticated actions (ac-14_odp), including identification of any additional unauthenticated actions that must be explicitly excluded | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 8h | — |
| Produce the AC-14 unauthenticated action register: exact permitted action names, scope (what is allowed/blocked), expected outcomes, and explicit exclusions (no authenticated content, no data retrieval beyond public content, no administrative functions) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| For each permitted action, document plain-language rationale for why identification/authentication is not required, including risk considerations and how misuse is prevented | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 16h | — |
| Record the decision in the system security plan (or equivalent system documentation artefact), ensuring auditors can trace permitted actions to the documented rationales and exclusions | Project Manager | Enterprise | 8h | — |
| Define and document validation approach for enforcement evidence per action (web/public routes, receive-only inbound call handling, fax ingestion restrictions, emergency maintenance bypass behaviour) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 12h | — |
| Execute validation and compile audit-ready evidence (test results/screenshots/log extracts) demonstrating unauthenticated users can only perform permitted actions and cannot reach authenticated-only workflows | Security Engineer | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Establish and document a change process trigger: any change to public routes/pages, call flows, fax handling, or emergency bypass requires re-review and re-approval of the AC-14 unauthenticated action list and rationales | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 12h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 96h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £0

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] List all system-specific actions that can be taken without authorisation, starting from **ac-14_odp**, and confirm that no other unauthorised actions are allowed.
- [ ] For every action that is allowed without authentication, document: the scope (what it covers), the expected result, any explicit things that are not allowed, and a clear plain-language reason for why it is permitted.
- [ ] Update the system security plan to include the list of actions that are allowed without authentication, along with the reasons for each.
- [ ] Run tests that use both valid and invalid access (without logging in) for every allowed action, and keep the results as audit-ready evidence.
- [ ] Document the emergency maintenance bypass procedure and confirm that audit records are created when the bypass is used.
- [ ] Add a change-control step that requires re-approval of the AC-14 unauthenticated action list whenever the related routes, user journeys, or bypass mechanisms are changed.

### AC-16 — Security and Privacy Attributes (Control)

This requirement ensures that every piece of information is correctly labelled for how it should be handled, who it relates to, and how long it should be kept—from the moment it is created until it is deleted. Without this, confidential or regulated information could be shared with the wrong people, kept for too long, sent without the right safeguards, or used without the required permission, which could cause legal, financial, and reputational harm.

The organisation must ensure that, in enterprise email, document repositories, operational technology systems, operational control workstations, and application programming interface (API) gateways, the system attaches and keeps the approved security and privacy labels with the information while it is stored, being processed, and while it is being sent.

The labels must use only approved values for:
- information classification (Confidential or Secret)
- export control status (Yes/No)
- retention period (30–365 days)
- encryption requirement (Required or Optional)
- access domain

Where relevant, the labels must also include privacy details about whether the information contains personal data, whether it includes a specific health identifier, whether consent or authorisation is in place, and whether the person is an employee, contractor, or customer.

The organisation must record and audit any changes to these labels, and review whether both the security and privacy labels are still suitable at least every 90 days.

##### Authoritative security/privacy labels bound to content with audit

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define permitted security/privacy attribute schema, allow-lists, and normalisation rules (including value ranges, defaults, and rejection/normalise behaviour) | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Design and implement authoritative label binding service/library (API contracts, validation, normalisation, and metadata envelope persistence) | Software Lead | Business Unit | 80h | — |
| Implement label propagation across lifecycle (versioning, transformations, exports, ingestion paths) with automated tests to ensure labels are carried through correctly | Platform Engineer | Business Unit | 64h | — |
| Enforce label persistence and integrity controls (anti-overwrite/blanking, privileged-only label change workflow, transmission context inclusion for messaging/API) | Security Engineer | Department | 56h | — |
| Implement immutable audit logging for label binding/changes (event schema, before/after capture, actor attribution, tamper-evident storage) | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Integrate audit events with enterprise SIEM/log pipeline and validate retention/correlation (including alerting hooks for invalid/unauthorised label changes) | Operations Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £12,000 |
| Build 90-day applicability review workflow with evidence capture (scheduled job, review UI/process hooks, exception handling and reporting) | Project Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Execute security/privacy assurance activities (threat modelling, code review, test execution, and audit readiness evidence pack for AC-16) | Quality Assurance Lead | Department | 40h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 392h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £12,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the approved security and privacy data rules (schema) and the approved lists (allow-lists) for the application’s covered data transfers
- [ ] Set up label binding when content is created or imported, using strict checks to ensure labels only use approved values and stay within allowed ranges.
- [ ] Ensure that label information (metadata) is carried through and kept unchanged across all processing steps, updates to versions, and exports.
- [ ] Add controlled authorisation for label changes and stop normal workflows from blanking or overwriting labels.
- [ ] Set up permanent (unchangeable) audit records for every label being linked and every label change, including the details of what changed (before and after), who made the change, when it happened, and the related object ID. Then send these records to the security information and event management (SIEM) system.
- [ ] Set up and run a scheduled 90-day process to check whether security and privacy labels apply, and record the results.


---

#### AC-16.1 — Dynamic Attribute Association (Enhancement)

This requirement is about ensuring the correct security and privacy rules automatically stay with the right people, accounts, and information as it is created, updated, combined, or moved. Without this, confidential or sensitive information could be given the wrong access permissions, or personal data could be handled in the wrong way—raising the risk of unauthorised disclosure, improper sharing, or keeping more personal information than allowed.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must use its Department of Defense (DoD) security classification and access authorisation rules (Secret or Confidential) and its DoD privacy notice and data minimisation rules (aligned to the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act (HIPAA), the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), and the Cybersecurity Maturity Model Certification (CMMC)). It must then apply the correct security and privacy labels automatically to:
- information system users and service accounts
- data stores such as files, databases, and message queues
- the individuals represented in records (for example patients, employees, and contractors)
- the personal data items those records relate to (such as HIPAA identifiers, GDPR personal data fields, and consent records)

The system must also keep these labels correct when access rights change, when data is reclassified, and when information is moved or combined. Proof of these changes must be recorded in system audit logs.

##### Event-driven security/privacy label recalculation for data and identities

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Define canonical security/privacy attribute model (schemas, data types, allowed values) and mapping rules from security/privacy policies, including “created and combined” composition logic | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 40h | — |
| Produce versioned policy-as-code repository (policy engine inputs/outputs, deterministic evaluation, unit tests, policy versioning/roll-back strategy) | Software Lead | Enterprise | 56h | — |
| Implement attribute service API + recalculation workflow (subject/object attribute computation, idempotency, dependency tracking, and deterministic recompute for affected entities) | Platform Engineer | Enterprise | 72h | — |
| Integrate event subscriptions for authoritative triggers (IAM/IdP changes, data lifecycle events, aggregation/combination jobs, privacy context/consent changes) with reliable delivery (retry/DLQ) | DevOps Lead | Enterprise | 64h | £25,000 |
| Implement write-back to target systems (storage metadata, database row/record attributes, message headers/metadata, identity attribute stores) with schema migrations and backward compatibility | Infrastructure Lead | Business Unit | 48h | £10,000 |
| Implement audit evidence generation (before/after diffs, trigger event, policy version, actor/service identity, tamper-evident retention) and integrate with SIEM/logging | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £15,000 |
| Enforce correct behaviour at access time (attribute-aware authorisation checks and/or enforcement gateway; remove reliance on cached/stale labels) | Security Engineer | Department | 56h | — |
| End-to-end testing and governance sign-off (test harness for composition scenarios, regression suite, performance/load tests, security/privacy validation, and release readiness) | Quality Assurance Lead | Enterprise | 48h | £8,000 |

**Total Estimated Effort**: 424h  
**Total Estimated Cost**: £58,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define a standard security and privacy attribute structure that covers the subjects and objects defined by the organisation.
- [ ] Turn the organisation’s security and privacy rules into version-controlled “policy as code”, including the rules for how “created and combined” outputs should be put together.
- [ ] Set up event notifications from identity and access management (IAM) and identity providers (IdPs), and from data platforms, so that attribute updates are automatically recalculated when access permissions change and when data is created, updated, or removed.
- [ ] Set up repeatable, consistent recalculation rules for both security and privacy information, for each individual record and for each personal data object.
- [ ] Store the calculated attributes back in the correct identity and data systems (for example, metadata, headers, and row fields), and make sure any later access checks use these updated values.
- [ ] For every recalculation, turn on audit logging that records the trigger event, the policy version, and the attribute values before and after the change.


---

#### AC-16.2 — Attribute Value Changes by Authorized Individuals (Enhancement)

This expectation is about ensuring that only the right people can change important access and privacy settings, and that every change can be traced back to who made it and when. Without this, someone could quietly change who is allowed to see information or change how personal data is handled, which could lead to unauthorised access, privacy breaches, or decisions being made using incorrect settings.

To meet this requirement, the organisation must keep an up-to-date list of named individuals (and any approved automated processes acting on their behalf) who are allowed to set or change security-related and privacy-related attribute values. It must also have clear, documented rules and procedures for how these changes are requested, approved, and carried out. The systems must be configured to block unauthorised attempts, and the system must record every successful change in audit logs, including the person (or approved process) responsible and the time the change was made.

##### Authorised attribute changes via step-up admin workflow with audit trail

- Category: Software
- Priority: Critical

| Task / Deliverable | Owner Role | Org Level | Effort (Hours) | Cost (GBP) |
|---|---|---:|---:|---:|
| Inventory security/privacy attributes and document all attribute write surfaces (API/UI/admin endpoints), including current authorisation behaviour and data model mappings | System Design Authority | Enterprise | 24h | — |
| Define attribute ownership and approval rules: attribute-to-role mapping for “Security Attribute Changers” and “Privacy Attribute Changers”, including minimum-change scope and segregation of duties | Compliance Manager | Enterprise | 32h | — |
| Configure enterprise IAM: create dedicated roles/groups, map attributes to roles, and implement phishing-resistant step-up authentication policy for attribute write operations | Cyber Security Lead | Enterprise | 40h | £3,000 |
| Implement server-side enforcement in the application: RBAC/ABAC checks on every attribute write request, deny-by-default for unauthorised identities, and ensure step-up status is validated before processing | Software Lead | Department | 56h | — |
| Build approved admin workflow integration: request/approval flow with justification capture, approver identity, and workflow reference; implement binding for automated processes acting on behalf of authorised individuals | Platform Engineer | Department | 64h | £6,000 |

| Execute test and assurance: unit/integration tests for authorisation/step-up denial, workflow approval gating, and audit completeness; run security regression and produce evidence pack for AC-16(2) | Quality Assurance Lead | Department | 32h | — |

**Total Estimated Effort:** 336h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £14,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Create an inventory of all security and privacy features and classify them, including the administrative and application programming interface (API) endpoints that can change them.
- [ ] Create separate identity and access management (IAM) roles or groups for “Security Attribute Changers” and “Privacy Attribute Changers”, and assign each role to the relevant attribute categories.
- [ ] Set up step-up authentication using phishing-resistant multi-factor authentication (MFA) for all actions that change privileged attributes.
- [ ] Integrate attribute changes into an approved workflow that records who requested and who approved the change, and ensures the action is carried out only by the authorised person.
- [ ] Carry out authorisation checks on the server for every request to change an attribute, and block any unauthorised attempts.
- [ ] Set up permanent (immutable) audit records for every successful change to an attribute. Each record must include who made the change (actor identity), the date and time (timestamp), the value before and after the change, what the change applied to (target), and the related workflow reference.
- [ ] Send audit events to security information and event management (SIEM) with integrity protections, and check that audit logs are kept for the required period and that access controls are in place.


---

#### AC-16.3 — Maintenance of Attribute Associations by System (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure the system always keeps the correct security and privacy labels attached to the right people and the right information, and that those label links cannot be quietly changed or corrupted. Without this, someone or an automated process could be incorrectly treated as allowed (or incorrectly blocked), confidential or sensitive information could be shared or kept in the wrong way, and required export or privacy handling could be bypassed without anyone noticing.

To meet this, the organisation must clearly define which security and privacy labels matter (for example, security clearance, need-to-know, health and data-protection tags, and export-control handling), which users, service accounts, and privileged roles they apply to, and which datasets, files, and policy or configuration items they must be linked to. The organisation must also ensure the system uses these labels for automated decisions, and that any change to the label links or to the underlying “known good” versions of key configuration and policy items is detected and flagged.

Finally, it must document how labels are applied, how the system is built and configured to keep them correct, include this approach in security and privacy plans, assign responsibility to relevant staff and developers, and keep evidence that integrity checks and baseline comparisons are working.

##### Signed attribute assertions and baseline-verified bindings for subjects/objects

**Total Estimated Effort:** 288h  
**Total Estimated Cost:** £36,000

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define the approved structure (schema) for security and privacy attributes, and how those attributes are linked to the authorised identifiers for the people (subjects) and systems or resources (objects) involved.
- [ ] Set up an attribute authority that issues short-lived, digitally signed statements confirming the link between a person (subject) or item (object) and their attributes.
- [ ] Set up enforcement points (authorisation and policy decision steps) so automated decisions for access, information sharing, and data retention use only verified information.
- [ ] Create and publish version-controlled “known good” baseline settings for attribute bindings and for security configuration or policy objects that are monitored for integrity.
- [ ] Set up integrity monitoring to spot any changes from the expected baseline and configure safe failure behaviour—deny or restrict access and send an alert—when a mismatch is detected.
- [ ] Provide audit-ready proof: records of each item’s lifecycle, logs showing signature checks were successful, baseline lists of expected files, and a history of alerts from integrity monitoring.

#### AC-16.4 — Association of Attributes by Authorized Individuals (Enhancement)

This requirement is about making sure the right people can correctly label information and accounts with the correct protection level and privacy meaning, and that the system keeps those labels attached to the correct records. Without this, sensitive material could be labelled incorrectly or not labelled at all, which could lead to unauthorised access, mishandling of regulated data, and confusion about who is allowed to see what.

To meet this expectation, the organisation must set out:
- which security labels are allowed (PUBLIC, CONFIDENTIAL, SECRET, plus compartment codes);
- which privacy tags are allowed (HIPAA role and GDPR role for people, and HIPAA PHI and GDPR personal data for information);
- which users and service accounts are allowed to assign or change these labels.

The system must allow authorised people (or automated processes acting on their behalf) to apply these labels to the correct targets, including files, directories, and network ports in regulated use, as well as on-premises document management items. It must also prevent invalid or unsuitable combinations.

For users with higher privileges, the system must help them choose correctly using prompts or automated categorisation where selected, check that their choices are valid, and record every change in an audit trail so the full history can be traced.

**Total Estimated Effort:** 280h

###### Actions Required for Compliance

- [ ] Define and implement a list of approved security labels and privacy tags, including which label and tag combinations are allowed for each type of object.
- [ ] Put authorisation checks on the server so that only authorised assigners (either users or approved service identities) can create, change, or remove attribute associations.
- [ ] Add user interface (UI) and application programming interface (API) workflows that prompt authorised users to choose from controlled options for security labels and privacy tags.
- [ ] Check that each combination is valid when it is first linked, and reject any invalid sets of attributes with clear error messages.
- [ ] Keep associations linked to the correct official subject and object identifiers, and prevent any mappings that are left without a match or are linked to the wrong item.
- [ ] Set up audit records for every change to an attribute link, including who made the change, what was changed (the target identifiers and attributes), the date and time, and whether it was done by a user or automatically. Then send these records to security information and event management (SIEM).

#### AC-16.5 — Attribute Displays on Objects to Be Output (Enhancement)

This expectation is about making sure that whenever the system prints or displays a document, it clearly explains what the information is, who it belongs to, and how it should be handled—without accidentally revealing sensitive details to the wrong person. Without this, someone could look at or photograph a screen, or read a printed page, and learn confidential or privacy-related information they are not authorised to see.

To meet this, the organisation must ensure that every document output includes the labels **Classification**, **Handling Caveats**, **Data Owner**, **System/Network**, **Release/Distribution**, and **Privacy Notice ID**, written in a way that a non-technical reader can understand. The system must show the complete values for these labels only when the viewer has authorised access to unmask them. Otherwise, it must show masked (redacted) values.

The organisation must document this approach in clear procedures, make sure the system is built and configured to follow them, assign responsibility to the right staff, and keep records proving that the correct labels were used and that the system showed masked versus full values as required.

##### Human-readable security/privacy attributes on all outputs

- Priority: Critical
